</p><p>Mathematical models to simulate dynamic processes of <a href="/wiki/Protein_folding" title="Protein folding">protein folding</a> and binding involve <a href="/wiki/Molecular_mechanics" title="Molecular mechanics">molecular mechanics</a>, in particular, <a href="/wiki/Molecular_dynamics" title="Molecular dynamics">molecular dynamics</a>.  <a href="/wiki/Monte_Carlo_method" title="Monte Carlo method">Monte Carlo</a> techniques facilitate the computations, which exploit advances in parallel and <a href="/wiki/Distributed_computing" title="Distributed computing">distributed computing</a> (for example, the <a href="/wiki/Folding@home" title="Folding@home">Folding@home</a> project<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scheraga2007_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scheraga2007-68">[68]</a></sup> which performs <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_modeling_on_GPU" title="Molecular modeling on GPU">molecular modeling</a> on <a href="/wiki/Graphics_processing_unit" title="Graphics processing unit">GPUs</a>). <i><a href="/wiki/In_silico" title="In silico">In silico</a></i> simulations discovered the folding of small α-helical <a href="/wiki/Protein_domain" title="Protein domain">protein domains</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Villin" title="Villin">villin</a> headpiece<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zagrovic2002_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zagrovic2002-69">[69]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a> accessory protein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herges2005_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Herges2005-70">[70]</a></sup>  Hybrid methods combining standard molecular dynamics with <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanical</a> mathematics explored the electronic states of <a href="/wiki/Rhodopsin" title="Rhodopsin">rhodopsins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffman2006_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffman2006-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Protein_disorder_and_unstructure_prediction">Protein disorder and unstructure prediction</span></h4>
<p>Many proteins (in Eucaryota ~33%) contain large unstructured but biologically functional segments and can be classified as <a href="/wiki/Intrinsically_disordered_proteins" title="Intrinsically disordered proteins">intrinsically disordered proteins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Predicting and analysing protein disorder is, therefore, an important part of protein structure characterisation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TompaFersht2009_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-TompaFersht2009-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrition">Nutrition</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Protein_(nutrient)" title="Protein (nutrient)">Protein (nutrient)</a></div>
<p>Most <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a> and plants can biosynthesize all 20 standard <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amino_acids" title="Amino acids">amino acids</a>, while animals (including humans) must obtain some of the amino acids from the <a href="/wiki/Diet_(nutrition)" title="Diet (nutrition)">diet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voet_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Voet-27">[27]</a></sup> The amino acids that an organism cannot synthesize on its own are referred to as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Essential_amino_acids" title="Essential amino acids">essential amino acids</a>. Key enzymes that synthesize certain amino acids are not present in animals — such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aspartokinase" title="Aspartokinase">aspartokinase</a>, which catalyses the first step in the synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Lysine" title="Lysine">lysine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methionine" title="Methionine">methionine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Threonine" title="Threonine">threonine</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aspartate" title="Aspartate">aspartate</a>. If amino acids are present in the environment, microorganisms can conserve energy by taking up the amino acids from their surroundings and <a href="/wiki/Downregulation_and_upregulation" title="Downregulation and upregulation">downregulating</a> their biosynthetic pathways.
</p><p>In animals, amino acids are obtained through the consumption of foods containing protein. Ingested proteins are then broken down into amino acids through <a href="/wiki/Digestion" title="Digestion">digestion</a>, which typically involves <a href="/wiki/Denaturation_(biochemistry)" title="Denaturation (biochemistry)">denaturation</a> of the protein through exposure to <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolysis</a> by enzymes called <a href="/wiki/Protease" title="Protease">proteases</a>. Some ingested amino acids are used for protein biosynthesis, while others are converted to <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> through <a href="/wiki/Gluconeogenesis" title="Gluconeogenesis">gluconeogenesis</a>, or fed into the <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid_cycle" title="Citric acid cycle">citric acid cycle</a>. This use of protein as a fuel is particularly important under <a href="/wiki/Starvation" title="Starvation">starvation</a> conditions as it allows the body's own proteins to be used to support life, particularly those found in <a href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrosnanJ_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-BrosnanJ-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>In animals such as dogs and cats, protein maintains the health and quality of the skin by promoting hair follicle growth and keratinization, and thus reducing the likelihood of skin problems producing malodours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson_1998_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watson_1998-75">[75]</a></sup> Poor-quality proteins also have a role regarding gastrointestinal health, increasing the potential for flatulence and odorous compounds in dogs because when proteins reach the colon in an undigested state, they are fermented producing hydrogen sulfide gas, indole, and skatole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Case_2010_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Case_2010-76">[76]</a></sup> Dogs and cats digest animal proteins better than those from plants but products of low-quality animal origin are poorly digested, including skin, feathers, and connective tissue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Case_2010_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Case_2010-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_and_etymology">History and etymology</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_molecular_biology" title="History of molecular biology">History of molecular biology</a></div>
<p>Proteins were recognized as a distinct class of biological molecules in the eighteenth century by <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Fran%C3%A7ois,_comte_de_Fourcroy" title="Antoine François, comte de Fourcroy">Antoine Fourcroy</a> and others, distinguished by the molecules' ability to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coagulate" title="Coagulate">coagulate</a> or <a href="/wiki/Flocculation" title="Flocculation">flocculate</a> under treatments with heat or acid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Noted examples at the time included albumin from <a href="/wiki/Egg_white" title="Egg white">egg whites</a>, blood <a href="/wiki/Serum_albumin" title="Serum albumin">serum albumin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fibrin" title="Fibrin">fibrin</a>, and wheat <a href="/wiki/Gluten" title="Gluten">gluten</a>.
</p><p>Proteins were first described by the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_people" title="Dutch people">Dutch</a> chemist <a href="/wiki/Gerardus_Johannes_Mulder" title="Gerardus Johannes Mulder">Gerardus Johannes Mulder</a> and named by the Swedish chemist <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius" title="Jöns Jacob Berzelius">Jöns Jacob Berzelius</a> in 1838.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mulder1938_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mulder1938-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hartley_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hartley-79">[79]</a></sup> Mulder carried out <a href="/wiki/Elemental_analysis" title="Elemental analysis">elemental analysis</a> of common proteins and found that nearly all proteins had the same <a href="/wiki/Empirical_formula" title="Empirical formula">empirical formula</a>, C<sub>400</sub>H<sub>620</sub>N<sub>100</sub>O<sub>120</sub>P<sub>1</sub>S<sub>1</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perrett2007_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perrett2007-80">[80]</a></sup> He came to the erroneous conclusion that they might be composed of a single type of (very large) molecule. The term "protein" to describe these molecules was proposed by Mulder's associate Berzelius; protein is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> word πρώτειος (<i>proteios</i>), meaning "primary",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> "in the lead", or "standing in front",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reynolds2003_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds2003-82">[82]</a></sup> + <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/-in#Suffix" title="wikt:-in">-in</a></i>. Mulder went on to identify the products of protein degradation such as the <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acid</a> <a href="/wiki/Leucine" title="Leucine">leucine</a> for which he found a (nearly correct) molecular weight of 131 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_mass_unit" title="Atomic mass unit">Da</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perrett2007_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Perrett2007-80">[80]</a></sup> Prior to "protein", other names were used, like "albumins" or "albuminous materials" (<i>Eiweisskörper</i>, in German).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>Early nutritional scientists such as the German <a href="/wiki/Carl_von_Voit" title="Carl von Voit">Carl von Voit</a> believed that protein was the most important nutrient for maintaining the structure of the body, because it was generally believed that "flesh makes flesh."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bischoff1860_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bischoff1860-84">[84]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Karl_Heinrich_Ritthausen" title="Karl Heinrich Ritthausen">Karl Heinrich Ritthausen</a> extended known protein forms with the identification of <a href="/wiki/Glutamic_acid" title="Glutamic acid">glutamic acid</a>. At the <a href="/wiki/Connecticut_Agricultural_Experiment_Station" title="Connecticut Agricultural Experiment Station">Connecticut Agricultural Experiment Station</a> a detailed review of the vegetable proteins was compiled by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Burr_Osborne_(chemist)" title="Thomas Burr Osborne (chemist)">Thomas Burr Osborne</a>. Working with <a href="/wiki/Lafayette_Mendel" title="Lafayette Mendel">Lafayette Mendel</a> and applying <a href="/wiki/Liebig%27s_law_of_the_minimum" title="Liebig's law of the minimum">Liebig's law of the minimum</a> in feeding <a href="/wiki/Laboratory_rat" title="Laboratory rat">laboratory rats</a>, the nutritionally <a href="/wiki/Essential_amino_acid" title="Essential amino acid">essential amino acids</a> were established. The work was continued and communicated by <a href="/wiki/William_Cumming_Rose" title="William Cumming Rose">William Cumming Rose</a>. The understanding of proteins as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polypeptide" title="Polypeptide">polypeptides</a> came through the work of <a href="/wiki/Franz_Hofmeister" title="Franz Hofmeister">Franz Hofmeister</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hermann_Emil_Fischer" title="Hermann Emil Fischer">Hermann Emil Fischer</a> in 1902.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> The central role of proteins as <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> in living organisms was not fully appreciated until 1926, when <a href="/wiki/James_B._Sumner" title="James B. Sumner">James B. Sumner</a> showed that the enzyme <a href="/wiki/Urease" title="Urease">urease</a> was in fact a protein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sumner1926_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sumner1926-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>The difficulty in purifying proteins in large quantities made them very difficult for early protein biochemists to study. Hence, early studies focused on proteins that could be purified in large quantities, e.g., those of <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egg_white" title="Egg white">egg white</a>, various <a href="/wiki/Toxin" title="Toxin">toxins</a>, and digestive/metabolic enzymes obtained from <a href="/wiki/Slaughterhouse" title="Slaughterhouse">slaughterhouses</a>. In the 1950s, the <a href="/wiki/Armour_and_Company" title="Armour and Company">Armour Hot Dog Co.</a> purified 1 kg of pure bovine pancreatic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ribonuclease_A" title="Ribonuclease A">ribonuclease A</a> and made it freely available to scientists; this gesture helped ribonuclease A become a major target for biochemical study for the following decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perrett2007_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-Perrett2007-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KendrewMyoglobin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="227" data-file-width="340" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/KendrewMyoglobin.jpg/250px-KendrewMyoglobin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ec/KendrewMyoglobin.jpg 1.5x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KendrewMyoglobin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_Kendrew" title="John Kendrew">John Kendrew</a> with model of myoglobin in progress</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Linus_Pauling" title="Linus Pauling">Linus Pauling</a> is credited with the successful prediction of regular protein <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secondary_structure" title="Secondary structure">secondary structures</a> based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bonding" title="Hydrogen bonding">hydrogen bonding</a>, an idea first put forth by <a href="/wiki/William_Astbury" title="William Astbury">William Astbury</a> in 1933.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pauling1951_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pauling1951-88">[88]</a></sup> Later work by <a href="/wiki/Walter_Kauzmann" title="Walter Kauzmann">Walter Kauzmann</a> on <a href="/wiki/Denaturation_(biochemistry)" title="Denaturation (biochemistry)">denaturation</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kauzmann1956_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kauzmann1956-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kauzmann1959_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kauzmann1959-90">[90]</a></sup> based partly on previous studies by <a href="/wiki/Kaj_Ulrik_Linderstr%C3%B8m-Lang" title="Kaj Ulrik Linderstrøm-Lang">Kaj Linderstrøm-Lang</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kalman1955_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kalman1955-91">[91]</a></sup> contributed an understanding of <a href="/wiki/Protein_folding" title="Protein folding">protein folding</a> and structure mediated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrophobic_core" title="Hydrophobic core">hydrophobic interactions</a>.
</p><p>The first protein to be <a href="/wiki/Protein_sequencing" title="Protein sequencing">sequenced</a> was <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Sanger" title="Frederick Sanger">Frederick Sanger</a>, in 1949. Sanger correctly determined the amino acid sequence of <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a>, thus conclusively demonstrating that proteins consisted of linear polymers of amino acids rather than branched chains, <a href="/wiki/Colloid" title="Colloid">colloids</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Cyclol" title="Cyclol">cyclols</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sanger1949_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sanger1949-92">[92]</a></sup> He won the Nobel Prize for this achievement in 1958.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lecture_1958_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lecture_1958-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first <a href="/wiki/Protein_structure" title="Protein structure">protein structures</a> to be solved were <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Myoglobin" title="Myoglobin">myoglobin</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Max_Perutz" title="Max Perutz">Max Perutz</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Kendrew" title="John Kendrew">Sir John Cowdery Kendrew</a>, respectively, in 1958.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Muirhead1963_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Muirhead1963-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kendrew1958_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kendrew1958-95">[95]</a></sup> As of  2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Protein&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the <a href="/wiki/Protein_Data_Bank" title="Protein Data Bank">Protein Data Bank</a> has over 126,060 atomic-resolution structures of proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlRCSB_Protein_Data_Bank_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlRCSB_Protein_Data_Bank-96">[96]</a></sup> In more recent times, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryo-electron_microscopy" title="Cryo-electron microscopy">cryo-electron microscopy</a> of large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macromolecular_Assembly" title="Macromolecular Assembly">macromolecular assemblies</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhou2008_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zhou2008-97">[97]</a></sup> and computational <a href="/wiki/Protein_structure_prediction" title="Protein structure prediction">protein structure prediction</a> of small protein <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Structural_domain" title="Structural domain">domains</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keskin2008_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keskin2008-98">[98]</a></sup> are two methods approaching atomic resolution.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Databases_and_projects">Databases and projects</span></h3>
<ul><li><a class="external text" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sites/entrez?db=protein" rel="nofollow">NCBI Entrez Protein database</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sites/entrez?db=structure" rel="nofollow">NCBI Protein Structure database</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.hprd.org/" rel="nofollow">Human Protein Reference Database</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.humanproteinpedia.org/" rel="nofollow">Human Proteinpedia</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://folding.stanford.edu/" rel="nofollow">Folding@Home (Stanford University)</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.pdbe.org/" rel="nofollow">Protein Databank in Europe</a> (see also <a class="external text" href="http://www.pdbe.org/quips" rel="nofollow">PDBeQuips</a>, short articles and tutorials on interesting PDB structures)</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.rcsb.org/" rel="nofollow">Research Collaboratory for Structural Bioinformatics</a> (see also <a class="external text" href="http://www.rcsb.org/pdb/static.do?p=education_discussion/molecule_of_the_month/index.html" rel="nofollow">Molecule of the Month</a>, presenting short accounts on selected proteins from the PDB)</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.proteopedia.org/" rel="nofollow">Proteopedia – Life in 3D</a>: rotatable, zoomable 3D model with wiki annotations for every known protein molecular structure.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20080608183902/http://www.expasy.uniprot.org/" rel="nofollow">UniProt the Universal Protein Resource</a></li></ul>
